## kimi

**Review Report: Nature Communications Manuscript**

**Manuscript Title:** Hardware-Aware Simulation of Organic Optoelectronic Compute-in-Memory Inference for Edge Vision  
**Authors:** Songqiao Li  
**Reviewer:** #2 (Expert in CIM/Analog AI, Organic Devices, and Edge Vision Architectures)  
**Date:** April 10, 2026

---

### 1. Summary

This manuscript presents a profile-driven simulation framework for evaluating organic optoelectronic compute-in-memory (CIM) inference using modern vision architectures (Tiny-ViT, ConvNeXt, ResNet-18). The core claim is that while organic devices offer attractive analog properties, their deployment viability is limited not by nominal quantization or simple stochastic noise, but by converter precision, hardware-instance overfitting, and nonlinear write dynamics. The authors propose "Ensemble Hardware-Aware Training" (HAT) to mitigate instance overfitting, demonstrating a recovery from 10.00% (chance level) to 86.37% accuracy on fresh hardware instances. The framework is positioned as a "materials-to-system decision bridge" enabling literature-derived device profiles to be evaluated for task-level deployment risks before chip fabrication.

---

### 2. Significance & Novelty

**Gap Addressed:** The manuscript correctly identifies a critical disconnect in the organic neuromorphic literature: device papers report conductance windows and retention, while system designers need to know if those specifications support modern vision transformers. Bridging this gap is timely and important.

**Contribution Type:** Primarily methodological. The "profile-driven substitution interface" allowing seamless swapping of literature-derived parameters is a practical contribution. However, the novelty relative to existing frameworks (AIHWKIT, CrossSim, NeuroSim) is nuanced. While the authors correctly note these tools are optimized for inorganic RRAM, the manuscript lacks rigorous quantitative benchmarking against them. The single ResNet-18 sanity check in Supplementary Section 1.7 (91.80% vs 96.88% digital) is insufficient to establish numerical equivalence or superiority.

**Impact Assessment:** The finding that standard HAT can catastrophically overfit to specific D2D realizations (10% accuracy) while Ensemble HAT recovers robustness is significant and counter-intuitive. This alone warrants NC consideration. However, the simulation-only nature and first-order approximations (no IR drop, no sneak paths, gradient-scaling NL model) limit the immediate impact for hardware designers seeking cycle-accurate predictions.

**Venue Suitability:** Suitable for *Nature Communications* provided the authors clarify the scope as a "first-order behavioral framework" rather than a predictive circuit emulator. The work sits at the intersection of materials science and computer architecture, fitting NC's interdisciplinary scope.

---

### 3. Technical Soundness

**Simulation Assumptions:** Generally justified, but with important caveats:
- **Differential Conductance Mapping:** The assumption of ideal common-mode cancellation (Section 1.4.1) and ideal scale recovery is acknowledged as a simplification. In practice, branch asymmetry would degrade performance further, meaning the results are optimistic bounds.
- **Retention Model:** The uniform double-exponential model is validated against state-dependent decay in Table S5 (difference <0.1 pp), supporting its use for this parameter regime.
- **Noise Injection:** Gaussian C2C/D2D models are standard, though real organic devices often exhibit non-Gaussian tails. The proportional noise mode (state-dependent) is appropriately handled.

**Critical Technical Issues:**

1. **ADC Energy Anomaly (Fig. 5):** The claim that ADC/DAC energy constitutes <0.1% of total inference energy (0.11 μJ / 274 μJ) is surprisingly low. While mathematically consistent with the 6-bit "knee" and hybrid architecture (digital attention dominates), this conflicts with conventional wisdom that ADCs dominate CIM energy. The authors must justify the ADC model (architecture, sampling rate, type—SAR? Flash?) or risk credibility. If the ADC is indeed negligible, the paper should explain why prior CIM literature emphasizes ADC bottlenecks.

2. **Catastrophic Instance Overfitting:** The collapse of Tiny-ViT V4 to 10.00% (exactly chance level for CIFAR-10) on fresh D2D instances is extreme. This suggests the model learned to exploit specific noise patterns as "signatures" rather than generalizable features. While Ensemble HAT fixes this, the standard HAT result implies the gradient updates are encoding D2D-specific artifacts. A deeper analysis (e.g., weight distribution divergence between instances) is needed to explain *why* transformers are more susceptible than ConvNeXt (which shows 71.6% in Fig. S2).

3. **Nonlinear Write Approximation:** The NL=2.0 boundary at 27.72% is described as a "limit of the gradient-scaling approximation." This is honest, but without comparing to a pulse-faithful model or actual device programming curves, readers cannot assess whether this is a methodology failure or a true physical limit.

4. **Statistical Rigor:** Table 2 reports "±" values for Tiny-ViT, but the supplement reveals these are Monte Carlo inference variations (10 runs), not training seed variations. For ConvNeXt C4 proportional HAT, the three-seed result (84.75±0.72%) differs significantly from the "best single run" (91.98%), indicating high training variance that should be reported for all regimes, not just when convenient.

**Missing Controls:** No ablation study on the effect of the photoresponse frontend (γ compensation) across different lighting conditions or datasets beyond the brief mention in Section 5.5.

---

### 4. Presentation & Narrative

**Story Arc:** Generally clear (Problem → Gap → Framework → Findings). However, the "Findings" section (Section 5) is dense and could benefit from subheadings separating "What Works" (Ensemble HAT, proportional noise recovery) from "What Fails" (fresh-instance transfer, nonlinear write).

**Abstract:** Accurate but slightly overstates certainty. Phrases like "quantization alone is often not the dominant limit" are supported, but "are the measured states... already good enough" implies experimental validation, whereas this is simulation-only.

**Figures:**
- **Fig. 3-4:** Clear and impactful. The "scale masking" effect is well visualized.
- **Fig. 5:** As noted, the ADC energy bar is suspiciously small and needs annotation explaining the model assumptions.
- **Fig. S2 (Supplement):** The Tiny-ViT bars are all labeled "10.0" making comparison impossible—should show error bars or distinct values if they differ.
- **Fig. S3:** Attention heatmaps are effective qualitative evidence.

**Length:** The main text at ~11 pages (excluding references) is appropriate, but the supplement is repetitive in places (Section 1.3.3 sensitivity tables show identical rows labeled as "stability results"—this formatting is confusing).

**Over-explained:** Section 2 (Related Work) spends excessive space on general organic device reviews. Condense to focus on CIM-specific prior art and simulation frameworks.

**Rushed:** Section 6.3 ("Task Complexity") raises the "HAT Data-Floor Hypothesis" but offers no mechanistic insight. The Flowers-102 failure needs deeper analysis (e.g., was LR tuning attempted?).

---

### 5. Missing References & Comparisons

1. **AIHWKIT Detailed Comparison:** Cite and benchmark against recent AIHWKIT ViT studies (e.g., "Analog training of ViT" papers from 2024-2025 if available). The supplement mentions a single ResNet sanity check; ViT-specific comparisons are absent.

2. **Recent Organic Array Papers:** The Liu et al. 2026 citation appears to be this year (or a preprint). Ensure all 2025 organic CIM array papers are cited, particularly those addressing variability (e.g., Zhang et al., *Microsystems & Nanoengineering* 2025; Cui et al., *Nature Nanotechnology* 2025).

3. **ViT Quantization Baselines:** The 6-bit ADC "cliff" should be compared to standard ViT INT8/INT4 quantization results (e.g., Liu et al. NeurIPS 2021, Li et al. NeurIPS 2022) to contextualize whether 6-bit is aggressive or conservative for vision transformers.

4. **Ensemble/D2D Resampling:** Cite related work on "noise annealing" or "stochastic depth" techniques that similarly randomize hardware instances during training (e.g., work by F. Cai, M. Rahimi Azghadi, or S. Yu on variability-aware training).

---

### 6. Specific Issues

**Line-by-Line/Structural:**

- **Abstract, Line 3:** "are the measured states... already good enough" — Change to "simulated states" or "literature-derived profiles" to avoid implying experimental validation.
- **Section 1, Line 2:** "April 10, 2026" — Ensure this date aligns with submission metadata.
- **Section 3.2:** "ideal calibrated digital rescaling" — Clarify if this implies negligible energy cost (as currently modeled) or if calibration energy should be added to Fig. 5.
- **Section 5.6:** "∼1.00× wall-clock overhead" — Ambiguous. Does this mean 1× (no overhead) or 2× (100% increase)? Clarify.
- **Table 2:** Missing error bars for ResNet-18. "—" for CIFAR-100/Flowers implies not tested, but brief explanation needed.
- **Section 5.9:** "Zhang 2026 OPECT" — If this is a concurrent submission or preprint, clarify status to avoid confusion.
- **Supplementary Section 1.3.3:** Table S3 shows identical accuracy values across all C2C variations (88.57%, 88.53%, etc.). While interpreted as D2D dominance, the formatting suggests a copy-paste error. Add a footnote confirming these are distinct runs.
- **Supplementary Fig. S2:** Tiny-ViT panel shows six green bars all labeled "10.0". If these represent different profiles, show variance. If identical, state "all profiles collapsed to chance level (~10%)".

---

### 7. Questions for Authors

1. **ADC Model Validation:** Your energy model assigns <0.1% to ADCs (Fig. 5), yet typical CIM literature reports ADCs as the dominant cost. What specific ADC architecture (SAR, Flash, Pipeline?) and conversion parallelism justifies this? Would a more realistic ADC model (including sampling energy for attention activations) change the 11.45× energy advantage significantly?

2. **Mechanism of Instance Overfitting:** Tiny-ViT collapses to exactly 10% (chance) on fresh D2D instances, while ConvNeXt maintains 71%. Is this because the transformer attention maps amplify specific D2D perturbations into global feature corruption? Can you visualize the attention entropy distribution across instances to prove this mechanism?

3. **Nonlinear Write Fidelity:** The NL=2.0 failure at 27% uses a gradient-scaling approximation. Given that organic devices often exhibit strong write asymmetry, how confident are you that this approximation preserves the ranking of different training recipes? Have you validated against a pulse-by-pulse programming model or actual device cycling data?

4. **Ensemble HAT Overhead:** You report "∼1.00× wall-clock overhead" for resampling D2D each epoch. Does this mean zero overhead, or 100% overhead (2× total time)? If zero, why does resampling not increase computation time? If Ensemble HAT incurs no overhead, why is it not the default training mode?

5. **Real-Device Calibration:** The framework is designed for "measured JSON profiles," yet all experiments use literature-derived proxies. Without a single real-device validation point, how should readers interpret the absolute accuracy numbers (e.g., 88.53% for Zhang profile) versus relative trends?

---

### 8. Overall Recommendation

**Recommendation:** **Minor Revision**

**Justification:** The manuscript addresses a genuine and timely gap between organic device characterization and system-level deployment viability. The "Ensemble HAT" solution to instance overfitting is a significant methodological advance with clear practical implications. The writing is clear, limitations are transparently discussed (Section 6.5), and the supplement provides extensive parameter provenance.

However, the manuscript requires revision to address:
1. **Validation Scope:** Clarify that this is a "first-order behavioral" tool, not a predictive circuit emulator, and temper claims about "deployment" accordingly.
2. **Statistical Rigor:** Report training-seed variance for all key results (not just ConvNeXt C4), not just Monte Carlo inference variance.
3. **Technical Anomalies:** Explain the surprisingly low ADC energy contribution or revise the model to include more realistic peripheral costs.
4. **Comparative Context:** Expand the comparison to AIHWKIT/CrossSim beyond the single ResNet sanity check, or acknowledge this as future work.

The 10%→86% recovery via Ensemble HAT is a compelling result that justifies NC publication, provided the authors clarify the simulation fidelity boundaries and address the statistical reporting gaps. The work will be valuable to both materials scientists seeking application contexts and architects evaluating emerging device technologies.

**Priority Revisions:**
- Add training-seed error bars to Table 2.
- Justify ADC energy model in Fig. 5 caption or methods.
- Explain the "1.00× overhead" wording in Section 5.6.
- Expand Section 2.2 to explicitly contrast with AIHWKIT's organic modeling capabilities (or lack thereof).

## deepseek

# Reviewer Report — Nature Communications  
**Manuscript Title:** Hardware-Aware Simulation of Organic Optoelectronic Compute-in-Memory Inference for Edge Vision  

---

## 1. Summary

This manuscript presents a PyTorch-based simulation framework for evaluating organic optoelectronic compute-in-memory (CIM) accelerators on vision workloads. The authors inject device non-idealities—quantization, cycle-to-cycle (C2C) and device-to-device (D2D) variability, retention decay, ADC distortion, and nonlinear write dynamics—into ResNet-18, ConvNeXt-Tiny, and Tiny-ViT-5M inference pipelines. The core claims are: (1) nominal conductance quantization is not the dominant accuracy bottleneck under canonical assumptions; (2) Ensemble Hardware-Aware Training (Ensemble HAT) dramatically improves fresh-instance transferability (from 10.00% to 86.37% for Tiny-ViT); and (3) the framework enables zero-shot deployment evaluation using literature-derived device profiles without code changes. The work is simulation-only and explicitly acknowledges that no physical chip measurements are presented.

---

## 2. Significance & Novelty

**Does this work address a meaningful gap?**  
Partially. The authors correctly identify that existing CIM simulators (AIHWKIT, CrossSim, NeuroSim) are primarily optimized for inorganic memories and do not natively incorporate organic-specific physics—particularly optoelectronic frontend nonlinearity, pronounced state-dependent retention, and asymmetric write dynamics. A framework that systematically exposes these organic-specific failure modes is indeed valuable. However, the manuscript overstates the novelty of the *simulation methodology itself*. Differential-pair mapping, STE-based quantization, Monte Carlo noise injection, and analytical energy profiling are standard practice in the CIM simulation literature. The contribution lies in the **curated parameter space** and the **systematic stress-test matrix**, not in algorithmic innovation.

**Is the contribution methodological, experimental, or both?**  
Methodological, with the caveat that "methodological" here means *parameter selection and experimental design*, not fundamental simulation advances. The profile-substitution interface is cleanly implemented but conceptually straightforward. The value is in the **structured comparison across architectures, tasks, and device regimes**, which provides actionable deployment guidance for organic-device researchers.

**Comparison to existing frameworks:**  
The authors provide a preliminary AIHWKIT comparison (ResNet-18/CIFAR-10, 4-bit, matched noise: digital 96.88% → analog 91.80%) and state that their simulator "exhibit[s] the same qualitative degradation trend." This is a minimal sanity check, not a rigorous validation. No comparison is made to CrossSim or NeuroSim under identical organic parameter sets. The claim that the framework is "tailored to expose... organic-specific physical non-idealities" is valid, but the paper does not demonstrate that *other simulators would fail to capture these effects* if properly parameterized. AIHWKIT, for instance, supports custom conductance models, retention, and write nonlinearity. The novelty therefore hinges on the **profile-driven workflow and the specific stress-test campaign**, not on unique simulation capabilities.

**Would Nature Communications readers find this impactful?**  
The paper is better suited for a **specialty venue** such as *Nature Communications Engineering*, *Advanced Intelligent Systems*, or *IEEE Transactions on Emerging Topics in Computing*. The simulation-only nature, combined with the absence of a definitive device-technology breakthrough, makes the work incremental for the broad *Nature Communications* readership. The most compelling result—the Ensemble HAT recovery from 10% to 86% fresh-instance accuracy—is a **training recipe** finding, not a hardware discovery. The case study on the Zhang OPECT profile (88.53% zero-shot transfer) is promising but relies on proxy noise parameters (σ_C2C = 2%, σ_D2D = 3%) that the authors themselves acknowledge as "transparent proxy estimates" rather than measured array distributions. This weakens the "profile-driven" claim.

**Recommendation on venue fit:**  
Major revision with stronger experimental validation, or transfer to a more specialized journal.

---

## 3. Technical Soundness

### 3.1 Simulation Assumptions: Physical Justification

The canonical organic profile is anchored to Vincze et al. [2] (conductance window ~10, τ₁ = 140 ms, τ₂ = 610 ms, A₀ = 0.6, symmetric writes NL = 1.0). The retention parameters are explicitly extracted; this is acceptable for a behavioral model. However, several assumptions are insufficiently justified:

- **Conductance window G_max/G_min = 10:** The text states this is "anchored to the scalable operation regime" but does not explain why 10 is chosen over the 47.3 value from the Zhang OPECT case study. Is 10 representative of typical organic memristors, or is it an arbitrary convenient value? The large discrepancy between canonical (10) and Zhang (47.3) profiles is noted but not discussed in terms of its impact on the "quantization is not dominant" claim.

- **Uniform vs. proportional noise:** The canonical profile assumes uniform C2C noise (σ = 5% of full range). The authors then introduce proportional noise (σ ∝ |G|) as a "stress test" and show catastrophic collapse of standard V4 (10.00%). However, they do not provide a **physical justification** for why proportional noise is more realistic for organic devices. The Zhang OPECT case study uses proportional noise, but the source reference [1] is only cited as providing a "proxy estimate." Is proportional noise the *expected* regime for organic electrochemical devices, or is it a worst-case scenario? This distinction matters for interpreting the Ensemble HAT result.

- **ADC resolution sweep:** The 6-bit ADC "cliff" (accuracy <30% at 4 bits, >80% at 6 bits) is a simulator-scoped finding, but the authors do not explain how ADC resolution interacts with the **conductance range** and **noise floor**. If G_max/G_min is large, a 4-bit ADC may be sufficient; if the range is narrow, higher precision is needed. The paper would benefit from a **joint sweep** of ADC bits and conductance window, rather than treating them as independent.

- **Nonlinear write modeling:** The implementation uses a "gradient-scaling approximation" where ∂L/∂G is scaled by a factor derived from an exponential behavioral model. This is a **first-order surrogate**, not a true pulsed programming simulation. The authors acknowledge this limitation in Section 6.5, but the main text (Section 5.6) presents the NL = 2.0 failure (27.72%) as a "hard boundary." This is misleading without stronger caveats. A pulsed programming model with state-dependent step sizes and write-verify loops might yield different recovery behavior.

### 3.2 Device-to-System Mapping Formulation

The differential-pair conductance mapping (Section 1.4.1) is correctly formulated and follows standard practice. However, two concerns:

- **Scale recovery idealization:** The paper states that "scale recovery [is] modeled as ideal calibrated digital post-processing rather than as an explicit physical peripheral circuit." This is a significant idealization. In a real system, scale factors must be stored, updated (due to drift), and applied via multipliers or gain stages. The energy overhead of this calibration is not included in the 273.94 μJ estimate. The authors note that routing overhead could reduce the gain from 11.45× to ~9.90×, but this still assumes ideal scale recovery. A sensitivity analysis with **gain calibration error** (e.g., ±5% mismatch) would strengthen the energy-accuracy trade-off discussion.

- **Positive/negative branch symmetry:** The differential mapping assumes identical nominal characteristics for G⁺ and G⁻ branches. The authors flag this as a limitation in Section 6.5, but do not explore its potential impact. Given that organic devices often exhibit **asymmetric programming dynamics** (NL_LTP ≠ NL_LTD), the assumption of symmetric branches may mask a significant error source.

### 3.3 Experimental Controls and Statistical Rigor

- **Baselines:** Digital FP32 baselines are provided (Table 1). However, a **quantized digital baseline** (e.g., INT8 or INT4 post-training quantization) is missing. This is crucial for isolating the effect of analog noise from the effect of reduced precision. The V2 experiments (4-bit hybrid, no explicit noise) partially fill this gap, but a pure digital 4-bit baseline would clarify whether the observed degradation is due to quantization or the analog mapping itself.

- **Seeds and reproducibility:** The paper mentions a "multi-seed campaign [that] is still being accumulated" (Supplementary Section 1.3.1). The main results for V4 and Ensemble HAT are based on a **single seed (42)** , with a rerun sanity check provided. For Nature Communications, a **multi-seed summary with mean ± std** is expected, not a single-seed placeholder. The three-seed reproducibility evaluation for ConvNeXt (84.75 ± 0.72%) is a step in the right direction, but the Tiny-ViT results (the paper's central claims) lack this rigor.

- **Monte Carlo evaluations:** The paper uses 10-run Monte Carlo for noisy inference and 5-run for transferability studies. These are adequate for variability estimation, but the confidence intervals in Table S4 are suspiciously narrow (e.g., [88.52, 88.62]% for 2% D2D). This suggests that the 10-run budget may be insufficient to capture the tail of the noise distribution. A **power analysis** or justification of the Monte Carlo sample size is needed.

- **Error bars:** Error bars are reported for most analog results (± std). However, the **energy estimates** (273.94 μJ, 11.45× gain) are presented as point values without uncertainty ranges. Given the idealizations (scale recovery, routing, ADC modeling), a **sensitivity analysis** with ±20% parameter variation should accompany the headline energy numbers.

### 3.4 Energy Estimation Concerns

The analytical energy model (Section 1.4.3) is high-level and lacks validation against circuit-level simulations or measured data. Specific issues:

- **E_cell value:** The energy per differential-pair accumulation is not stated. What is the assumed value, and from where is it derived? The 11.45× gain is referenced to Horowitz's 2.5 pJ/MAC for FP32, but the analog E_cell must be justified. For organic devices, reported switching energies vary widely (fJ to pJ). The paper should explicitly state the assumed E_cell and provide a literature citation.

- **ADC/DAC energy:** The model charges ADC energy per output element and DAC per input element, with "behavioral DNL/INL modeling." No details are provided on the ADC architecture (SAR? slope?), sampling rate, or energy model. A 6-bit ADC at 100 MS/s consumes vastly different energy than a 10-bit ADC at 1 MS/s. The 6-bit cliff finding is central to the paper's conclusions, so the ADC energy assumptions must be transparent.

- **Digital attention energy:** The finding that digital attention accounts for 57.9% of total energy (Fig. 5) is plausible but depends on the assumed efficiency of the QKᵀ and softmax implementation. Are these operations computed on a dedicated digital coprocessor? What is the assumed energy per operation? The paper cites Li et al. [28] for INT8 ViT energy (2.0–4.0 mJ), but the hybrid stack estimate (273.94 μJ) is ~10× lower. This discrepancy should be reconciled.

### 3.5 Retention Model Validation

The comparison between uniform and state-dependent retention (Table S5) shows <0.1 pp accuracy difference, leading the authors to conclude that the uniform model is "sufficient for the present regime." This is a **single-checkpoint validation** on the Ensemble HAT model. It does not test whether state-dependent retention would matter for **standard HAT** models or **non-HAT** baselines. The conclusion is scoped appropriately in the text, but the abstract and main narrative do not convey this limitation.

---

## 4. Presentation & Narrative

### 4.1 Story Arc

The paper is organized around four "deployment-facing questions" stated in the introduction. This is an effective framing. However, the execution is uneven:

- **Question 1** (Is quantization dominant?) is answered in Section 5.2 with the "scale masking" observation. The explanation is plausible but the term "scale masking" is introduced without a rigorous definition or quantitative support. The authors state that digital scale recovery "rescales effective analog perturbations below the 4-bit LSB." This is a **hypothesis**, not a proven mechanism. A simple analysis comparing the noise standard deviation (σ_C2C × conductance range) to the quantization step size would clarify whether this explanation holds.

- **Question 2** (What fails first as tasks get harder?) is addressed via the cross-dataset results (Fig. 3–4). The Flowers-102 failure is noted, but the causal mechanism is left ambiguous. The authors propose a "data-floor hypothesis" but acknowledge that domain shift and hyperparameter mismatch are alternatives. This is intellectually honest but leaves the section unresolved.

- **Question 3** (Does deployment collapse on fresh hardware?) is the strongest narrative thread, with the 10% → 86% Ensemble HAT recovery serving as the paper's headline finding. The story is compelling and well-supported by Supplementary Fig. S2 and the Zhang case study.

- **Question 4** (Which stress regimes can be retrained around?) is partially answered by the proportional-noise recovery (97.37%) and nonlinear-write failure (27.72%). The narrative would benefit from a **summary table or decision tree** that maps device parameters to expected accuracy outcomes and recommended training strategies.

### 4.2 Abstract Accuracy

The abstract states: "Ensemble HAT raises fresh-instance Tiny-ViT accuracy from 10.00% to 86.37 ± 1.54%." This is accurate and well-supported. However, the abstract also claims that the framework "injects these non-idealities directly into the forward path of ResNet-18, ConvNeXt-Tiny, and Tiny-ViT-5M backbones." This is **true for inference**, but the HAT training is performed with the same injection. The abstract does not clarify that the results are simulation-only, which could mislead readers expecting hardware validation. A phrase such as "in simulation" or "behavioral modeling" should be added.

### 4.3 Figure Quality

- **Main figures (1–5):** Adequate. Fig. 1 (Hybrid stack) is clear. Fig. 2 (Weight-to-conductance mapping) is standard but useful. Fig. 3–4 (Cross-dataset accuracy) are readable but could benefit from error bars on the digital baselines (which have none, being single-run). Fig. 5 (Energy breakdown) is informative.

- **Supplementary figures:** Fig. S1 (Noise/ADC sweep) is critical but appears only in the supplement. Given the 6-bit ADC cliff's importance, this figure should be **promoted to the main text**. Fig. S2 (Fresh-instance transfer) is also central and should be in the main paper. Fig. S3 (Attention heatmaps) is qualitative; the entropy numbers provided in the text are more compelling and should be included in a main-text figure.

### 4.4 Length and Pacing

The main text is ~11 pages, which is appropriate for Nature Communications (typically 6–8 pages for main text, plus methods and supplementary). The **Results section (Section 5)** feels rushed, with many findings compressed into short paragraphs. The **Discussion (Section 6)** is stronger and more reflective. Some material in the Discussion (e.g., the "Scale Masking" effect explanation in 6.1) belongs in the Results section, where the phenomenon is first introduced.

### 4.5 Specific Clarity Issues

- **Acronyms:** The paper uses "HAT" (Hardware-Aware Training) extensively, which is fine, but also introduces "Ensemble HAT" without a clear definition in the main text. Supplementary Section 1.1 explains that Ensemble HAT "keeps the canonical V4 recipe but resamples the fixed D2D realization at each epoch." This definition should be in the main text.

- **V1–V8 notation:** The Tiny-ViT experimental variants (V1–V8) are defined in Supplementary Section 1.1. The main text refers to V2, V3, V4, V6 without redefining them, forcing the reader to flip to the supplement. A concise table in the main text summarizing the variants would greatly improve readability.

- **"Canonical" vs. "physical-extension" studies:** The distinction between the "canonical family" (V1–V6) and "physical-extension studies" (V4_proportional_HAT, V4_NL2_HAT, etc.) is useful but introduced late. Clarifying this taxonomy earlier would help readers navigate the dense results.

---

## 5. Missing References or Comparisons

### 5.1 Important Omissions

- **Organic CIM array demonstrations:** The paper cites Hu et al. [9] (IEEE TED 2023) on an energy-efficient organic device array, but does not engage with its reported array-level metrics (yield, variability, endurance). A comparison of the simulator's assumed σ_D2D = 10% to the measured variability in [9] would strengthen the parameter provenance.

- **Vision Transformer quantization:** The paper cites PTQ4ViT [24], Q-ViT [25], and FQ-ViT [26] but does not compare the analog degradation to the accuracy drop reported in these works for **digital low-precision ViT**. For instance, FQ-ViT reports ~1–2% accuracy loss for 4-bit quantization on ImageNet. The Tiny-ViT V2 result (97.39%, down from 98.06%) is comparable, suggesting that analog noise (5% C2C) adds minimal additional penalty. This comparison should be made explicit.

- **State-dependent retention in organic devices:** The paper anchors its retention model to Vincze et al. [2], which reports DNTT-based transistors. Other organic memristor studies (e.g., PEDOT:PSS, pentacene) exhibit different retention characteristics. A brief survey of retention time constants across organic materials would contextualize the chosen τ₁ = 140 ms, τ₂ = 610 ms values.

- **Analog ViT accelerators:** Several recent works propose ReRAM-based Transformer accelerators with hybrid analog-digital partitioning (e.g., ReTransformer, ViT-ReRAM). The paper's finding that digital attention dominates energy (57.9%) echoes these studies. Citing and comparing to these works would strengthen the energy analysis.

### 5.2 Missing Comparison: AIHWKIT/CrossSim Under Organic Parameters

As noted earlier, the paper's AIHWKIT comparison is minimal (one checkpoint, 5 runs). A more systematic comparison—e.g., running the canonical V4 regime in AIHWKIT and comparing the resulting accuracy, energy, and runtime—would substantiate the claim that the present framework offers organic-specific advantages. Without this, the novelty of the *simulator itself* is difficult to assess.

---

## 6. Specific Issues (Line-by-Line)

Given the PDF format without line numbers, I will reference by section and paragraph.

- **Section 3.1, paragraph 2:** "This choice is consistent with the present organic hardware literature, which demonstrates regular static array programming much more naturally than fully dynamic interaction engines." This is an assertion without citation. Provide references that explicitly discuss the difficulty of dynamic programming in organic arrays.

- **Section 3.2:** The phrase "scale recovery [is] modeled as an ideal calibrated digital rescaling step" should be accompanied by a footnote or parenthetical noting that calibration overhead is not included in energy estimates.

- **Section 5.2:** "We attribute this to the scale-masking effect: digital scale recovery rescales effective analog perturbations below the 4-bit LSB, absorbing much of the nominal conductance noise into quantized weight bins." This is a **central claim** but is presented as an interpretation rather than a demonstrated mechanism. Either provide a quantitative analysis (e.g., comparing σ_noise to quantization step size after rescaling) or soften the language to "we hypothesize."

- **Section 5.4:** "Tiny-ViT V4 collapses to chance level (10.00%) when evaluated on arrays with different fixed D2D realizations." The phrase "chance level" for CIFAR-10 is 10.00%, which is correct. However, the supplementary figure (Fig. S2) shows the collapse visually. Consider adding a brief explanation: *Why* does standard HAT overfit to the specific D2D pattern? Is it because the training process learns to compensate for the fixed spatial noise via weight adjustments that are brittle to changes?

- **Section 5.8:** The energy estimate "273.94 μJ per Tiny-ViT-5M inference" is presented with four significant figures. Given the modeling idealizations, two significant figures (e.g., 270 μJ) are more appropriate. The same applies to the 11.45× gain.

- **Section 6.1, "Scale Masking" paragraph:** "The observation that V2 survives..." The sentence is incomplete in the provided PDF (cuts off mid-thought). This appears to be a formatting artifact from the PDF extraction. Ensure the full text is present in the submission.

- **Supplementary Table S2:** The Zhang 2026 OPECT case study lists σ_C2C = 2% as a "proxy estimate from 8-cycle repeatability." Is 2% the standard deviation of conductance over 8 cycles at a fixed target state? Clarify the extraction method. For σ_D2D = 3%, the table notes it is a "conservative conductance-domain proxy from the reported ~1% V_th spread." The mapping from V_th spread to conductance spread is not obvious and should be justified.

- **Supplementary Table S3–S4:** The sensitivity sweep shows **identical rows** across different C2C values (e.g., 1% C2C and 8% C2C both yield 88.57% accuracy at 2% D2D). The text explains this as a "stability result" where "per-forward C2C sampling noise remains sub-resolution." This is a **critical finding** that should be highlighted in the main text, as it reinforces the "scale masking" claim. However, the explanation is buried in the supplement. Move this to the main Results section.

---

## 7. Questions for Authors

1. **Parameter validation:** The canonical organic profile uses G_max/G_min = 10, σ_C2C = 5% (uniform), σ_D2D = 10%, and symmetric writes (NL = 1.0). Can you provide a **quantitative justification** for each of these values from the cited literature (Vincze et al. [2] and others)? Specifically, how were the 5% and 10% noise figures derived? Are they representative of **array-level** measurements or **single-device** characterization?

2. **Ensemble HAT mechanism:** Ensemble HAT resamples the D2D mask at each epoch during training. This is a form of **data augmentation in the weight space**. Have you considered whether **digital dropout** or **weight noise injection** during training would achieve a similar regularization effect, without requiring analog-specific simulation? In other words, is the Ensemble HAT benefit specific to analog D2D variability, or is it a general robustness improvement against weight perturbations?

3. **ADC resolution vs. conductance range:** The 6-bit ADC cliff finding assumes a fixed conductance range (G_max/G_min = 10). If the range were larger (e.g., 47.3 as in the Zhang case study), would the required ADC resolution decrease? A joint sweep of ADC bits and conductance window would clarify whether this is a **device-technology constraint** or a **system-design trade-off**.

4. **Profile-driven substitution validation:** The case study uses the Zhang OPECT profile with σ_C2C = 2% and σ_D2D = 3%. These are "transparent proxy estimates." Have you considered **measuring these parameters directly** from the published data in [1] (e.g., extracting conductance distributions from the array micrographs in Fig. 3h and Supp. Fig. 8)? Without direct extraction, the "profile-driven" claim rests on assumed proxies, which weakens its impact.

5. **State-dependent retention beyond Ensemble HAT:** Table S5 shows that uniform vs. state-dependent retention yields <0.1 pp difference for the **Ensemble HAT** checkpoint. Would the same hold for **standard HAT** (V4) or **non-HAT** (V3) models? The conclusion that uniform retention is "sufficient for the present regime" should be tested across the full experimental matrix, or explicitly scoped to Ensemble HAT.

---

## 8. Overall Recommendation

**Recommendation: Major Revision**

This manuscript presents a well-structured simulation campaign that exposes important failure modes in organic CIM deployment, particularly the fragility of standard HAT to fresh D2D instances and the promise of Ensemble HAT as a corrective. The profile-substitution workflow is a useful contribution to the organic-device community. However, the work falls short of Nature Communications standards in several critical areas:

1. **Simulation-only with weak validation:** The absence of any physical hardware measurements is not disqualifying for a methods-focused paper, but the *validation of the simulation assumptions* is insufficient. The canonical parameters are anchored to one primary reference [2], with several values described as "proxy estimates" or "behavioral assumptions." The AIHWKIT comparison is minimal. The paper needs either (a) stronger parameter extraction from published array data, or (b) a more extensive cross-validation against established inorganic simulators to establish the framework's quantitative reliability.

2. **Statistical rigor:** The headline Tiny-ViT results rely on single-seed training, with a multi-seed campaign described as "still being accumulated." For Nature Communications, a complete multi-seed evaluation with confidence intervals is expected at the time of submission.

3. **Energy model transparency:** The 11.45× energy gain is a central selling point, yet the underlying assumptions (E_cell, ADC/DAC architectures, digital coprocessor efficiency) are not disclosed. The energy model needs a detailed exposition, either in the main text or as a dedicated supplementary section.

4. **Clarity and completeness:** The V1–V8 notation is confusing without a main-text table. The "scale masking" mechanism is asserted without quantitative support. The Flowers-102 failure is left unresolved. These narrative gaps can be addressed in revision.

**If the authors can address these concerns—particularly by completing the multi-seed campaign, providing a transparent energy model with sensitivity analysis, and strengthening the parameter validation—the manuscript would represent a solid contribution to the CIM simulation literature. However, in its current form, it does not meet the threshold for a high-impact, broad-interest journal like Nature Communications.**

**Alternative venues to consider if the revisions prove extensive:** *Nature Communications Engineering* (more focused on methods/tools), *Advanced Intelligent Systems* (strong track record in neuromorphic simulation), or *IEEE Transactions on Computer-Aided Design of Integrated Circuits and Systems* (for the CIM/EDA community).

---

*End of Review*

## doubao

# Reviewer #2 Report: Hardware-Aware Simulation of Organic Optoelectronic Compute-in-Memory Inference for Edge Vision
*Submitted to Nature Communications*

---

## 1. Summary
This manuscript presents a profile-driven, first-order behavioral simulation framework tailored for organic optoelectronic compute-in-memory (CIM) edge vision inference, addressing a critical gap between materials-level organic synaptic device characterization and system-level deployment validation on modern vision models. The core claim is that this framework enables seamless substitution of literature-derived or measured device parameters to quantify task-level accuracy and energy tradeoffs, without modifying core training/evaluation code. Through systematic evaluation on Tiny-ViT, ConvNeXt-Tiny, and ResNet-18 across three vision datasets, the authors identify that standard hardware-aware training (HAT) suffers from severe hardware-instance overfitting, while their proposed Ensemble HAT raises fresh-instance accuracy from chance level (10.00%) to 86.37 ± 1.54% on CIFAR-10. They further show that severe write nonlinearity remains a hard performance boundary even with retraining, while converter precision and task complexity dominate deployment bottlenecks over nominal quantization. This is a simulation-only study with no new device fabrication or hardware measurement results.

## 2. Significance & Novelty
### Strengths
- **Meaningful Field Gap**: This work directly addresses a longstanding unmet need in the organic neuromorphic community: existing mature CIM simulators (AIHWKIT, CrossSim, NeuroSim) are calibrated for inorganic resistive memories, and do not natively support organic-specific non-idealities (optoelectronic frontends, dual-exponential retention decay, state-dependent noise, strong write nonlinearity) or hybrid analog/digital Vision Transformer (ViT) deployment. Most prior organic device studies stop at small-network validation on idealized mappings, with no systematic assessment of deployment risks on state-of-the-art edge vision models.
- **Dual Methodological and Experimental Contribution**: The primary contribution is methodological: a modular, PyTorch-based simulation framework with a standardized profile interface for plug-and-play device parameter substitution, paired with an automated profile fitting pipeline for measured device data. The experimental contribution is a rigorous, multi-dimensional ablation study that quantifies the relative impact of different non-idealities, identifies hardware-instance overfitting as a critical failure mode for ViT on analog CIM, and demonstrates Ensemble HAT as an effective mitigation.
- **Broad Impact for NC Readers**: Nature Communications serves a cross-disciplinary audience spanning materials science, device engineering, circuit design, and machine learning. This work builds a transparent, accessible bridge between materials-level device characterization and system-level deployment: it enables materials researchers to rapidly evaluate how their device performance metrics translate to real-world vision task accuracy, and provides system designers with a tool to prioritize device optimization targets. This cross-domain utility aligns strongly with NC’s scope.

### Limitations
- **Incremental Relative to Inorganic CIM Frameworks**: While the organic-specific customization is novel, the core simulation methodology (differential conductance mapping, straight-through estimator (STE) for HAT, Monte Carlo noise evaluation) builds on well-established practices from inorganic CIM simulators. The paper does not fully delineate how the framework’s core architecture advances beyond state-of-the-art inorganic toolkits beyond organic-specific feature additions.
- **Venue Suitability**: This work is appropriate for Nature Communications with major revisions. It is impactful for the broader neuromorphic community, but requires additional validation of the framework’s generality, more rigorous comparison to related work, and clearer separation of simulation predictions from hardware-verified results to meet NC’s standards.

## 3. Technical Soundness
### Strengths
- **Physically Justified Simulation Assumptions**: All core device parameters are anchored to peer-reviewed literature, with full parameter provenance and sensitivity analysis documented in the supplementary information (SI). The authors explicitly label proxy estimates and analytical assumptions, and validate key model choices: 2D sensitivity sweeps for cycle-to-cycle (C2C)/device-to-device (D2D) noise, and a head-to-head comparison of uniform vs. state-dependent retention decay (showing <0.1 percentage point accuracy difference in the tested regime).
- **Correct Device-to-System Mapping**: The weight-to-differential-conductance mapping, noise injection (fixed D2D mismatch, per-forward-pass C2C variation, state-dependent proportional noise), dual-exponential retention model, and STE gradient scaling for nonlinear writes are all formulated consistently with mainstream analog CIM simulation best practices, and aligned with reported organic device physics.
- **Adequate Controls and Statistical Rigor**: The authors include full FP32 digital baselines across three model architectures and three datasets of increasing complexity, with systematic ablations for quantization, noise type, ADC bit width, HAT strategy, and frontend compensation. Most key results include mean accuracy and standard deviation from Monte Carlo runs, with error bars reported for all stochastic evaluations.
- **Transparent Limitation Disclosure**: The authors explicitly flag out-of-scope effects (IR drop, sneak paths, temperature dependence, differential branch asymmetry) and quantify the potential impact of unmodeled routing overhead on energy estimates, avoiding overreach in their claims.

### Critical Technical Concerns
1. **Insufficient Multi-Seed Statistical Validation**: The authors note that the full multi-seed campaign for the canonical V4 regime is "still being accumulated", with only a single-seed sanity check reported. For core results (including the Ensemble HAT fresh-instance accuracy and nonlinear write failure boundary), only limited multi-seed data is provided (e.g., 3 seeds for ConvNeXt proportional-noise HAT). This raises concerns about whether the reported results are driven by favorable stochastic initialization, rather than robust model behavior.
2. **Incomplete Model Validation**: The "profile-driven" core claim is only validated with two literature-derived device profiles (canonical organic and Zhang 2026 OPECT). There is no demonstration of the framework’s generality across a broader range of organic device stacks, nor validation against measured hardware data (even from published literature) to confirm that simulation predictions match real-world array behavior.
3. **Oversimplified Peripheral and Array Modeling**: The ADC model only sweeps bit width, with no modeling of real-world non-idealities (differential/integral nonlinearity, sampling noise, aperture error) that dominate readout accuracy in practical CIM systems. Array-level effects (IR drop, sneak paths) are entirely omitted, with only a qualitative note of their potential impact; for organic devices with lower conductance than inorganic RRAM, IR drop effects are likely more severe, and their absence may lead to overly optimistic accuracy and energy estimates.
4. **Energy Estimation Limitations**: The analytical energy model provides only an upper-bound estimate, with no validation against published measured energy data from real organic CIM arrays or digital edge accelerators. The calibration overhead of the post-array scale recovery step is treated as ideal, with no costed peripheral circuitry, which may inflate the reported 11.45x energy reduction relative to the FP32 baseline.
5. **Narrow Scope of Retention Model Validation**: The uniform vs. state-dependent retention comparison is only performed on the Ensemble HAT checkpoint, with no validation on standard HAT or non-HAT models where retention decay may cause more severe accuracy degradation. The authors acknowledge this scoped validation, but it limits the generalizability of their claim that the uniform retention model is sufficient.

## 4. Presentation & Narrative
### Strengths
- **Clear Story Arc**: The manuscript follows a logical, compelling narrative: it opens with the core deployment gap for organic CIM, introduces the profile-driven framework as the solution, presents systematic experimental results to revise the expected failure hierarchy for organic CIM, and concludes with actionable insights for both materials and system researchers.
- **Accurate Abstract**: The abstract faithfully reflects the paper’s core contributions, key results, and limitations, with no overstatement of claims. It clearly communicates the framework’s purpose and the most impactful findings (Ensemble HAT performance, nonlinear write boundary) to both specialist and non-specialist readers.
- **Appropriate Length for NC Format**: The main manuscript is 13 pages, with supplementary material for detailed methods and extended results, which aligns with Nature Communications’ length guidelines. The discussion of limitations is thorough and balanced, avoiding overclaiming.

### Weaknesses
- **Redundant and Unpolished Figures**: Figures 3 and 4 in the main manuscript present overlapping cross-dataset accuracy results, with redundant information that could be consolidated into a single panel. Supplementary Figure S2 has a truncated accuracy axis with unclear labeling, and Figure S5 has cluttered, hard-to-interpret annotations for dark current and gamma values. The attention heatmaps in Supplementary Figure S3 are included without statistical context beyond a single entropy measurement, limiting their utility.
- **Imprecise Context in Results Sections**: Multiple key results are reported without explicit dataset or model context (e.g., "V2 attains 97.39% under standard uniform noise", "Tiny-ViT V4 collapses to chance level (10.00%)"), forcing readers to infer the experimental conditions from surrounding text.
- **Rushed Methodological Details**: Core modeling details (STE gradient scaling for nonlinear writes, differential conductance mapping rules, energy profiler implementation) are largely deferred to the supplementary material, with only cursory descriptions in the main manuscript. This makes the paper hard to follow for readers who do not review the SI in full, particularly non-specialist NC readers.
- **Inconsistent Notation**: There are minor but persistent notation inconsistencies: the main manuscript uses σD2D/σC2C, while the SI alternates between σD2D/σC2C and σC2C/σD2D; literature references have inconsistent year labeling (e.g., Vincze 2025 in the main manuscript vs. Vincze 2026 in the SI); and the OPECT device profile is attributed to "Zhang 2026" in the SI but "Liu 2025" in the main manuscript references.

## 5. Missing References or Comparisons
1. **Organic CIM Simulation Precedents**: The manuscript only cites Alibart et al.’s early 2016 work on organic memristive perceptron simulation, and omits more recent 2024–2026 studies on system-level organic CIM simulation, which are required to contextualize the novelty of this work.
2. **Multi-Instance HAT for Analog CIM**: The hardware-instance overfitting problem and Ensemble HAT mitigation are presented without reference to prior work on multi-instance/multi-noise HAT for inorganic analog CIM, which has explored similar strategies to improve cross-array transferability. The authors must cite these works and clarify the incremental advance of their Ensemble HAT approach for organic devices.
3. **ViT-on-Analog-CIM State of the Art**: The manuscript cites low-bit ViT quantization work, but omits recent 2025–2026 studies on ViT deployment for analog CIM, which have identified similar fragility to attention layer noise and proposed mitigation strategies. These comparisons are required to contextualize the authors’ ViT-specific findings.
4. **Optoelectronic Frontend Compensation**: The inverse-gamma compensation for photoresponse nonlinearity is presented without citation to prior work on gamma correction for neuromorphic vision sensors and optoelectronic synapse arrays, which have explored similar linearization strategies and noise tradeoffs.

## 6. Specific Issues
1. **Table Inconsistencies**: Main manuscript Table 1 reports ResNet-18 FP32 accuracy as 94.98%, while Table 2 reports the same value as 94.88% with no explanation. The asterisk on the ConvNeXt-Tiny Flowers-102 accuracy (33.22%*) in Table 1 is not defined in the table caption.
2. **Unclear Result Context**: Main manuscript Section 5.2, 5.4, and 5.9 report key accuracy results without explicit dataset labeling (all are CIFAR-10 results, but this is not stated in the text).
3. **Supplementary Table Clarity**: Supplementary Table S3 has numerically identical rows across all C2C values; while the authors explain this in the text, a note must be added to the table caption to avoid readers interpreting this as a copy-paste error.
4. **Overreaching Claim in Conclusion**: The conclusion states the framework enables "evaluating sensitivity before full chip availability", but the framework has not been validated against measured chip data, so this claim is unsupported.
5. **Reference Year Inconsistencies**: Multiple references have mismatched years between the main manuscript and SI (Vincze, Liu/Zhang OPECT work), which must be corrected to match the final published versions of the cited papers.
6. **Unjustified IR Drop Estimate**: Section 6.5 cites ReRAM benchmarks to estimate 1–3% accuracy degradation from IR drop for arrays >256×256, but organic devices have lower conductance and more severe IR drop effects; this estimate must be qualified as a lower bound for organic devices.
7. **Missing Operator Mapping Rationale**: Section 3.1 states depthwise convolutions remain digital, but does not briefly explain the core rationale (poor crossbar utilization) in the main text, only citing prior work.

## 7. Questions for Authors
1. Your core finding that standard HAT suffers from catastrophic hardware-instance overfitting is impactful, but the Ensemble HAT results are only validated for a single D2D mismatch value (10% in the canonical regime). Have you evaluated Ensemble HAT’s generalization performance across the full range of D2D mismatch values (2% to 15%) tested in your sensitivity sweep? Is there a D2D threshold beyond which Ensemble HAT is no longer effective?
2. The "profile-driven" interface is the central methodological contribution of this work, but it is only validated with two literature-derived device profiles. Have you tested the framework’s generality across a broader set of published organic synaptic device stacks? Do you plan to validate the framework’s predictions against measured array data from a fabricated organic CIM system?
3. You identify severe write nonlinearity (NL=±2) as a hard performance boundary with the current STE gradient-scaling approximation. Have you explored custom nonlinearity-aware STE formulations or specialized training strategies to mitigate this failure mode? Is this boundary fundamental to organic device physics, or a limitation of your current training methodology?
4. Your energy model reports an 11.45x upper-bound energy reduction relative to an FP32 baseline, but does not compare to state-of-the-art INT8 digital edge accelerators (the de facto standard for edge vision deployment). How does your hybrid analog/digital system’s estimated energy compare to measured results from commercial INT8 edge NPUs/MCUs for Tiny-ViT inference?
5. You note that the full multi-seed campaign for the canonical V4 regime is still in progress. Will you be able to provide complete multi-seed results for all core experiments (canonical HAT, Ensemble HAT, nonlinear write stress tests) in a revised version of the manuscript?

## 8. Overall Recommendation
**Recommendation: Major Revision**

This manuscript presents a highly impactful, cross-disciplinary contribution that addresses a critical gap in the organic neuromorphic and analog CIM fields. The proposed profile-driven simulation framework fills a clear need for a tool that bridges materials-level organic device characterization and system-level deployment validation on modern edge vision models, with rigorous ablation studies that revise our understanding of the dominant bottlenecks for organic CIM inference. The identification of hardware-instance overfitting as a critical failure mode for ViT on analog CIM, and the Ensemble HAT mitigation strategy, are novel findings with broad relevance to the neuromorphic hardware community. The work aligns strongly with Nature Communications’ mission to publish high-impact, cross-disciplinary research that advances multiple fields.

That said, the manuscript requires major revisions to meet Nature Communications’ rigorous standards for technical validity, contextual novelty, and transparency. The most critical required changes are: (1) complete multi-seed validation for all core experimental results to ensure statistical robustness; (2) full contextualization of the work against prior organic CIM simulation, multi-instance HAT, and ViT-on-CIM studies, with clear delineation of the manuscript’s incremental advances; (3) quantitative assessment of the impact of unmodeled array-level non-idealities (IR drop, sneak paths) and peripheral non-idealities on the reported accuracy and energy results; (4) correction of all notation, table, and reference inconsistencies, and improved clarity of experimental context in the results sections; and (5) validation of the profile-driven framework across a broader set of published organic device profiles to demonstrate its generality.

If the authors can fully address these concerns in a revised manuscript, this work will be an excellent fit for Nature Communications, and will serve as a foundational tool for the organic neuromorphic community for years to come.

## ds-txun

Manuscript Review for "Hardware-Aware Simulation of Organic Optoelectronic Compute-in-Memory Inference for Edge Vision"

1. Summary

The paper presents a behavioral simulation framework designed to bridge the gap between the physical characterization of organic optoelectronic synaptic devices and the system-level performance evaluation of modern vision models deployed in analog compute-in-memory (CIM) systems. The core claim is that this framework provides a structured, profile-driven tool to answer deployment-facing questions such as the impact of specific device non-idealities, the effectiveness of hardware-aware training (HAT), and the limits of analog robustness, particularly for transformer-based models like Tiny-ViT. The authors demonstrate the framework's utility by using literature-derived device profiles, revealing critical bottlenecks (e.g., hardware instance overfitting, ADC precision) and showing that ensemble-based HAT can mitigate some, but not all, failure modes (e.g., severe non-linear writing dynamics).

2. Significance & Novelity

Addressing a Gap: The work addresses a meaningful and timely gap. While excellent CIM simulators exist (AIHWKIT, CrossSim, NeuroSim), they are calibrated for inorganic memristors. This paper's focus on organic device specifics (photoresponse, state-dependent retention, profile substitution) and on a hybrid transformer deployment scenario is a valuable contribution.

Nature of Contribution: The contribution is primarily methodological. The authors do not present new device fabrication or measurement data. Instead, they build a flexible simulation and evaluation pipeline. The "profile-driven" approach for technology substitution is a central and compelling idea.

Comparison to Existing Frameworks: The paper correctly positions its work relative to established simulators. The novelty lies in the organic-specific physical modeling (optics, retention) and the explicit analysis of profile transferability. However, the claim of serving as a "transparent materials-to-system decision bridge" would be stronger with validation against measured device data, not just literature-derived priors.

Suitability for Nature Communications: The paper tackles a cross-disciplinary topic (materials, devices, systems, algorithms) of high interest. The profile-driven concept is innovative and the findings on transformer fragility are important. However, the simulation-only nature and the reliance on proxy estimates for key parameters (e.g., Zhang 2026 noise values) weaken the case for NC, which typically expects stronger experimental validation or a more definitive, field-advancing conclusion. The work may be better suited for a high-impact but more methods/application-focused venue like IEEE Transactions on Computers or Advanced Intelligent Systems, unless the authors can substantially strengthen the connection to real, measured hardware.

3. Technical Soundness

Simulation Assumptions & Mapping: The core modeling approach (differential conductance mapping, STE-based HAT, double-exponential retention) is well-grounded and aligns with common practices in analog-AI simulation. The use of a "canonical organic profile" anchored to Vincze et al. 2025 provides a reasonable baseline. The key weakness is the reliance on proxy estimates (e.g., for Zhang 2026's C2C/D2D noise) and the first-order approximation of nonlinear writes (NL_LTP/LTD as gradient scaling). While acknowledged as a limitation, this makes concrete performance predictions (e.g., the 88.53% for the Zhang profile) more speculative than definitive.

Experimental Controls: The experimental design is comprehensive. The use of multiple architectures (ResNet, ConvNeXt, Tiny-ViT) and datasets (CIFAR-10/100, Flowers-102) is a strength. The inclusion of FP32 baselines, quantized controls (V2), and various HAT regimes provides good ablation. The multi-seed and Monte Carlo evaluations are appropriate. However, the "first completed fresh rerun" for the V4 sanity check (Suppl. 1.3.1) is not statistically robust; a multi-seed summary is needed for the main claims.

Error Bars & Statistics: Monte Carlo standard deviations are reported for key results, which is good. However, confidence intervals or statistical significance tests for comparisons (e.g., between uniform and state-dependent retention in Table S5) are missing, making it hard to judge if the <0.1 pp difference is meaningful.

Specific Technical Concerns:
•   Quantization & Noise: The scale recovery/masking effect is interesting but its generality across architectures and noise types needs more discussion. The collapse under proportional noise is a critical finding.

•   ADC Modeling: The 6-bit "cliff" is a crucial result. The model seems behavioral; a brief description of the ADC energy/accuracy trade-off model is needed in the main text or a clear reference to the supplement.

•   Energy Estimation: The energy model is correctly framed as a first-order, upper-bound estimate. The 11.45x gain is useful for directional insight, but the omission of routing, control, and scale-recovery calibration overhead is a significant simplification that should be more prominently caveated in the abstract and results summary.

4. Presentation & Narrative

Story Arc: The narrative is clear and follows a logical progression: problem (device-system gap) → proposed solution (profile-driven simulator) → application to answer specific deployment questions → findings and limitations. The four-question structure is effective.

Abstract & Results: The abstract accurately reflects the work. The phrase "upper-bound-like estimate" for energy is appropriately cautious.

Figures & Length: The main manuscript text is well-structured and of appropriate length for NC. A major issue is that the provided document excerpts lack the main figures (Figs. 1-4, S1-S7 are referenced but not visible). From the text descriptions, the figures seem essential. The supplementary tables are detailed. Some result paragraphs (e.g., Sec 5.2, 5.6) are quite dense and could benefit from tighter writing or summary tables.

Section Balance: The "Discussion" section (Sec. 6) is strong and insightful, correctly framing the limitations. Section 1.3 in the Supplement (Parameter Provenance) is excellent and should be a model for simulation papers. Section 1.8 on the automated fitting pipeline feels slightly out of place as it describes future utility rather than employed methods.

5. Missing References or Comparisons

The related work section is comprehensive regarding CIM simulators and organic devices. However, a few areas could be strengthened:
•   ViT-on-Analog Hardware: While Liu et al. 2021 (Post-training quantization for ViT) is cited, more recent work on quantizing and deploying ViTs on analog hardware (e.g., "ViTALiTy: High-Speed Inference of Vision Transformers via a Token Pruning-based Crossbar Array" in ISCA 2024, or "TinyViT: Fast and Accurate Vision Transformers on Edge Devices" in ECCV 2024) should be discussed to better contextualize the transformer-specific findings.

•   Advanced HAT/Noise-Robust Training: Comparisons to more advanced robust training techniques for analog noise (beyond standard HAT), such as noise-injection during training with varying distributions or regularization methods specifically for device mismatch, would bolster the discussion on ensemble HAT as a solution.

•   Organic CIM Array Demonstrations: The references are good, but a 2024/25 paper by Fanqing Zhang et al. on "Large-scale high uniform optoelectronic synapses array" (Microsyst. Nanoeng., 2024) could be a relevant addition for state-of-the-art array performance.

6. Specific Issues

•   Line ~(Main, near end of 5.1): "Tiny-ViT-5M reaches 98.06% on CIFAR-10 via transfer learning" but Table 1 says 97.48%. Inconsistent. Please verify and correct.

•   Main Doc, Sec 5.8: "The analytical energy model estimates 273.94μJ per Tiny-ViT-5M inference, corresponding to an upper-bound 11.45x reduction relative to the FP32 digital reference (3.14 mJ, estimated from 2.5 pJ per FP32 MAC[1])." The reference [1] (Horowitz, 2014) is for a general computing energy problem, not a specific FP32 MAC energy. A more appropriate reference for a modern digital accelerator (e.g., TPU, GPU) energy per op should be used, or the assumption (2.5 pJ/FP32 MAC) should be explicitly justified.

•   Suppl., Sec 1.3.1: "Because the full multi-seed campaign is still being accumulated..." This phrasing is inappropriate for a final submission. The results should be based on a complete, statistically sound set of runs.

•   Notation: In the main text Table 2, some accuracy values have error bars (e.g., 97.39±0.05) and some do not (e.g., 94.88). Consistency is needed. Also, define what "V3", "V4", etc., are in the table caption or a footnote.

•   Figure References: The text frequently refers to Supplementary Figures (S1, S2, S5, S6) that are not provided in the review material. Their descriptions sound critical for understanding the ADC sweep, fresh-instance results, and frontend analysis. These must be included and of publication quality.

•   Typos/Minor:

    ◦   Main, Abstract: "localibra-tion" -> "localization" (Suppl, Fig S4 caption).

    ◦   Main, Sec 3.2: "mathe-matical" has a hyphen in the middle.

    ◦   Suppl., Sec 1.3.2: "ampli-tude" has a hyphen in the middle.

7. Questions for Authors

1.  Parameter Provenance & Uncertainty: The Zhang 2026 profile uses proxy estimates for σ_C2C and σ_D2D. The sensitivity sweep (Table S3) shows robustness to these proxies for the Ensemble HAT checkpoint. How would you bound the potential error in your primary conclusions (e.g., the 88.53% result) given that other un-swept parameters (conductance range, n_states) are also estimates? Could you provide a discussion on the overall uncertainty propagation from literature extraction to system accuracy?
2.  Non-Linear Write Modeling: The NL=2.0 stress test leads to a "hard boundary" at ~28% accuracy. Your model uses a gradient-scaling approximation. How might a more physical, pulse-based non-linear write model (e.g., with asymmetric saturation) change this boundary? Is the observed failure likely a limitation of your HAT recipe or a more fundamental constraint for analog Transformers?
3.  Hardware Instance Overfitting: The collapse of standard HAT (V4) on fresh instances is a striking result. Does your analysis reveal why Transformers are so susceptible to this compared to CNNs in your setup? Is it related to attention mechanism sensitivity, layer norms, or the pre-training/fine-tuning paradigm?
4.  Energy Model Realism: The 11.45x energy gain is an upper bound. Your discussion mentions adding 10-50% routing overhead reduces gains to 9.90-11.10x. Given that scale recovery, data marshaling, and control logic for hybrid execution are non-trivial, can you provide a more realistic, literature-based estimate of the expected overhead for a feasible system, and what the consequent likely energy gain would be?
5.  Frontend Compensation: The inverse-gamma compensation helps for high γ but amplifies noise for γ<1. For a real system operating under varying illumination, how would you decide whether to enable this compensation? Is a gain-control mechanism needed, and what would be its cost?

8. Overall Recommendation

Major Revision (leaning towards Reject if the simulation-experiment gap is deemed too large for NC).

Justification:
The manuscript presents a thoughtfully constructed and potentially useful simulation framework for an important emerging domain. The profile-driven concept, the focus on transformers, and the identification of hardware-instance overfitting as a critical failure mode are significant strengths with clear value to the community.

However, for Nature Communications, the simulation-only nature combined with the reliance on proxy/estimated device parameters significantly weakens the impact. The central value proposition is a "bridge" from device characteristics to system performance, but the bridge is only tested with literature-derived estimates, not actual measured data from a fabricated array. Key claims, such as the performance of the "Zhang 2026 profile," are therefore predictive rather than validated. The energy savings are presented as an optimistic upper bound. While the authors are transparent about these limitations, the work in its current form reads more like a sophisticated simulation methodology paper than a study yielding concrete, experimentally-grounded insights suitable for NC's broad audience.

To be considered for NC, the authors need to:
1.  Strengthen Validation: Ideally, apply the framework to at least one set of measured device data (even from a prior publication) and show that the simulation predictions align with or can explain observed hardware/system results. Failing that, a much more rigorous uncertainty quantification for all predictions based on proxy data is required.
2.  Clarify Claims: The abstract and conclusion should more forcefully state that all results are simulation-based predictions, not experimental measurements. The energy gain should be explicitly labeled as a "theoretical upper bound" in the abstract.
3.  Address Technical Gaps: Provide the missing multi-seed results for key claims, include confidence intervals, fix inconsistencies, and integrate the missing figures.

If the authors cannot provide a stronger experimental or validation link, I would recommend submission to a prestigious but more methods-oriented journal (e.g., IEEE Transactions on Computer-Aided Design of Integrated Circuits and Systems, Advanced Intelligent Systems), where the methodological contribution would be highly valued.

## mimo

### **审稿报告：Nature Communications**

**稿件标题：** Hardware-Aware Simulation of Organic Optoelectronic Compute-in-Memory Inference for Edge Vision
**稿件编号：** （未提供）

---

### **1. Summary**
本文提出了一种基于配置文件驱动的、一阶行为模拟框架，旨在评估有机光电突触器件在存算一体（CIM）边缘视觉推理中的系统级部署可行性。该框架将文献或测量得到的器件参数（如导电范围、噪声、非线性写入、保持衰减）注入到PyTorch训练/推理流程中，用于评估ResNet-18、ConvNeXt-Tiny和Tiny-ViT-5M在CIFAR-10/100和Flowers-102数据集上的准确率和能效。核心论点是：在典型条件下，量化本身并非主要瓶颈，而ADC精度、任务复杂度、新硬件实例迁移以及更丰富的器件物理（如强非线性写入）构成了主要的部署风险。集成硬件感知训练（Ensemble HAT）被证明是提升跨实例鲁棒性的关键方法。

### **2. Significance & Novelty**
*   **意义：** 本文精准地抓住了有机电子学领域一个关键且悬而未决的“决策鸿沟”：器件级的优异特性（多级导电、低功耗）能否转化为系统级的可行性？这项工作通过提供一个从材料特性到任务性能的结构化评估工具，直接回应了系统设计者面临的核心问题。对于CIM和神经形态硬件社区，这是一个及时且重要的贡献。
*   **新颖性：** 贡献主要是方法论上的，兼具一定的实验性（模拟实验）。
    1.  **框架特异性：** 与AIHWKIT、CrossSim、NeuroSim等主要针对无机存储器（如RRAM）的成熟模拟器不同，本文框架专门针对有机光电器件的独特物理特性（如光电前端响应、状态相关保持、非对称非线性写入）进行了定制。这是其核心新颖点。
    2.  **“配置文件驱动”的接口：** 将器件特性参数化并模块化为可替换的“配置文件”，使得同一框架能无缝对接文献数据或未来实测数据，无需修改核心代码。这增强了工具的通用性和前瞻性。
    3.  **聚焦Transformer部署：** 将分析扩展到ViT这类对噪声和量化更敏感的现代架构，并深入探讨了其特有的故障模式（如硬件实例过拟合、注意力分散），这超越了以往多数有机器件研究中对小网络的验证。
*   **对比与定位：** 与现有CIM模拟器相比，本文并非在数值精度或电路建模上竞争，而是提供了一个更侧重于**部署风险早期评估**和**材料-系统协同设计**的专用透镜。对于《自然·通讯》的读者，尤其是从事柔性/有机电子、神经形态工程和边缘AI交叉领域的研究人员，这项工作具有显著的影响和启发意义。它更适合作为一篇方法学论文发表在《自然·通讯》上，而非纯粹的器件物理或电路会议。

### **3. Technical Soundness**
*   **模拟假设：** 大部分一阶行为假设（高斯噪声、双指数衰减、STE梯度近似）在探索性系统研究中是合理且可接受的。作者在讨论（6.5节）和补充信息中诚实地指出了理想化之处（如忽略IR降、潜通路、差分对称性），这体现了良好的学术规范。
*   **映射正确性：** 从权重到差分导电对，再到量化、噪声注入和数字缩放恢复的映射流程，在概念上是正确的，并遵循了该领域的常见做法。
*   **实验控制：** 实验设计全面，包含了数字基线、不同架构、多数据集、多种噪声/物理应力测试以及消融研究（如标准HAT vs. 集成HAT）。提供了随机种子和确定性设置以追求可复现性，这是值得肯定的。
*   **统计报告：** 关键结果（如集成HAT的准确率）报告了多次运行的均值和标准差，符合规范。
*   **潜在问题：**
    1.  **能量估算：** 能量模型是高度抽象的分析模型（如2.5 pJ/FP32 MAC），且未计入互连开销。虽然作者通过敏感性分析展示了结论的稳健性，但将这种“上界估计”与INT8数字加速器的实际芯片功耗进行对比（如6.4节），可能略显牵强，尽管结论在方向上可能是正确的。
    2.  **比例噪声的定义：** 正文中“比例噪声”的表述可以更精确。从补充信息看，它指的是噪声标准差σ与瞬时导电值|G|成正比。这与“均匀噪声”（噪声归一化到全范围）形成关键区别，但在正文摘要和结果部分的对比中，这一定义的解释稍显不足。
    3.  **ADC“悬崖”的普适性：** 观察到的6-bit ADC精度悬崖是模拟器范围内的一个发现。需要更明确地强调，其具体阈值高度依赖于模型、任务和所假设的ADC行为模型（DNL/INL），不应被过度泛化。

### **4. Presentation & Narrative**
*   **故事弧线：** 非常清晰。从器件-系统脱节的问题出发，提出配置文件驱动的模拟框架作为桥梁，通过四个部署导向的问题组织实验，最终得出关于瓶颈、鲁棒性和工具价值的结论。逻辑流畅。
*   **摘要：** 准确反映了核心方法、关键发现（集成HAT的提升、非线性写入的硬边界）和研究定位（决策桥梁而非芯片预测器）。
*   **图表：** 图1（系统栈示意图）和图2（权重映射流程）清晰有效。图3和图4支持了主要论点。补充图表（如热图、保持曲线）提供了必要的细节。整体达到出版质量。
*   **篇幅与节奏：** 对于NC格式，正文篇幅适中。各部分详略得当，没有明显感到仓促或过度解释的章节。补充信息很好地收纳了方法细节和附加结果。
*   **改进建议：** 在“结果”部分（5.2节）首次引入“比例噪声”时，可简要重申其与“均匀噪声”的区别，以增强可读性。在讨论“比例噪声HAT”恢复到97.37%时，应更突出强调这仅在**训练与评估噪声分布匹配**时成立，这与同节提到的“分布匹配的鲁棒性”论点紧密相连。

### **5. Missing References or Comparisons**
*   **有机阵列演示：** 近期已有一些展示大规模、高均匀性有机光电突触阵列用于视觉神经网络的工作（例如，*Microsystems & Nanoengineering* 11, 42 (2025)），这些可作为本文框架潜在验证对象的更近实例加以引用，以强化其现实相关性。
*   **ViT量化与硬件：** 可以补充引用一些专门探讨ViT在低精度或模拟硬件上挑战的最新工作，以进一步支撑关于Transformer敏感性的讨论。
*   **与AIHWKIT的数值对比：** 补充信息中提到了与AIHWKIT的初步对比，但在正文讨论中（如与成熟框架对比时）可更明确地引用此结果，以定位本框架的模拟保真度水平。

### **6. Specific Issues**
*   **表述精确性：**
    *   第5.6节：“proportional-noise HAT” 和 “severe write non-linearity (NL = 2.0)” 是两种不同的应力测试。虽然分开讨论，但在总结句“分布匹配的比例噪声重训练可以恢复到97.37 ± 0.05%，但强非线性写入仍然是一个硬边界”中，将两者并列对比，可能让读者误以为是在同一种应力下的不同结果。建议稍作厘清。
    *   第5.8节：“upper-bound 11.45x reduction” – “上界”的表述是准确的，但在正文中首次出现时，可考虑更明确地说明这是基于理想化能量模型的估计。
*   **图表/表格：**
    *   表2中，ConvNeXt-Tiny在Flowers-102上的数字基线（33.22%）带有星号(*)，但在表格标题或脚注中未解释（补充信息中提到是“单次运行估计”）。应在主文表格中补充脚注。
*   **其他：**
    *   第6.1节提到“比例噪声HAT”的恢复，但未明确指出其与“集成HAT”策略的关系。集成HAT是否也使用了比例噪声？文中V4_Ensemble_HAT的描述是“保持标准V4方案但每轮重采样D2D”，而V4_proportional_HAT是另一个实验。需要明确集成HAT实验（获得86.37%）具体使用的是哪种噪声模型（应为标准均匀噪声），以避免混淆。

### **7. Questions for Authors**
1.  **关于配置文件验证：** 框架的长期愿景是用实测数据替换文献先验。除了补充信息中提到的自动拟合工具，是否有计划（或已经进行）用一组实际制备的有机光电突触器件的测量数据来闭环验证该模拟框架？如果有，能否简要分享初步观察到的模拟与实测系统性能之间的主要差异来源？
2.  **关于“比例噪声”的物理对应：** 将噪声建模为与瞬时导电值成正比（σ ∝ |G|）是一个重要的应力测试。这种关系在多大程度上反映了实际有机器件中的物理机制（例如，是否与载流子数涨落有关）？作者是否认为这是一种比均匀噪声更“物理”的模型？
3.  **关于集成HAT的计算开销：** 文中提到集成HAT引入了约1.00倍的额外训练时间。这种开销主要来自每轮重采样D2D掩码，还是其他因素？对于更复杂的模型或更大的数据集，这种开销是否可扩展？是否探索过更高效的多实例训练策略（例如，每个batch中混合不同实例）？
4.  **关于“模拟天花板”：** 作者正确地指出了模拟与物理实现之间的差距（如IR降）。根据现有文献（如引用的ReRAM基准），如果加入这些效应，预计对Tiny-ViT准确率的额外惩罚是否会改变本文关于“量化非主导瓶颈”的核心结论？还是说这些效应主要影响绝对精度，但不改变相对趋势？
5.  **关于可复现性细节：** 目标是通过种子和配置实现“执行轨迹一致性”。在实际操作中，使用PyTorch和CUDA时，确保完全可复现性颇具挑战。作者是否遇到了跨不同硬件/驱动版本（即使使用相同种子）结果不一致的情况？如果有，是如何处理的？

### **8. Overall Recommendation**
**推荐决定：小修（Minor Revision）**

**理由：** 本文提出了一种有价值且定义明确的方法学工具，填补了有机光电CIM领域从器件到系统评估的关键空白。研究设计扎实，结果清晰，讨论诚实且富有洞察力，对社区具有明确的贡献。稿件质量总体上符合《自然·通讯》的标准。需要进行的修改主要是技术性的，旨在提升表述的精确性、澄清潜在的概念混淆（如不同应力测试的关系）、并加强与现有工作的对比。这些问题可以通过仔细的文字修订和有限的补充说明来解决，无需进行大规模的额外实验。

## Qwen

Here is the peer review report for the manuscript, structured according to your requirements.

---

### **1. Summary**
This manuscript presents a hardware-aware simulation framework designed to evaluate the deployment viability of Organic Optoelectronic Compute-in-Memory (CIM) systems for edge vision tasks. The authors utilize this framework to analyze the resilience of modern vision architectures—specifically Tiny-ViT, ResNet-18, and ConvNeXt-Tiny—under the non-ideal physical constraints of organic devices, such as retention decay, nonlinear write dynamics, and state-dependent noise. The core claim is that while quantization is not the primary bottleneck, severe challenges arise from hardware-instance overfitting and nonlinear write dynamics, which can be mitigated through specific hardware-aware training (HAT) strategies like Ensemble HAT.

### **2. Significance & Novelty**
*   **Meaningful Gap:** The paper addresses a critical gap. While organic synaptic devices are promising, there is a lack of system-level simulation tools that translate low-level device physics (e.g., specific retention laws, optical frontend nonlinearities) into task-level performance metrics for modern vision models.
*   **Contribution Type:** This is a methodological contribution with significant systems-level analysis. It bridges materials science and computer architecture.
*   **Comparison to Existing Tools:** Unlike NeuroSim or CrossSim (which are mature for inorganic RRAM), this framework is specialized for organic optoelectronics, incorporating unique features like optical frontend compensation and specific retention models for organic materials.
*   **Nature Communications Fit:** The topic is highly relevant to NC's broad audience. The intersection of novel hardware, energy efficiency, and AI algorithms is strong. However, the simulation-only nature might be a point of contention, but the thoroughness of the analysis likely justifies the venue.

### **3. Technical Soundness**
The methodology is generally rigorous but has specific areas requiring scrutiny:

*   **Simulation Assumptions:**
    *   **Strengths:** The use of double-exponential retention models anchored to literature (Vincze et al.) is physically justified. The inclusion of "Ensemble HAT" to address instance overfitting is a novel and necessary methodological twist.
    *   **Weaknesses:** The assumption of "ideal calibrated digital rescaling" for scale recovery ignores the significant hardware overhead of calibration circuits in real analog systems. This is a common simplification but should be acknowledged as a limitation.
*   **Mapping & Noise:**
    *   The differential conductance mapping is standard. However, the treatment of noise as purely additive Gaussian (even in proportional mode) might not fully capture the heavy-tailed distributions often seen in organic ionic migration.
*   **Experimental Controls:**
    *   **Adequacy:** The use of multiple seeds (e.g., in ConvNeXt reproducibility checks) and Monte Carlo sweeps is good practice.
